Revelation

22

1AND he shewed me a pure river of water of life, clear as crystal, proceeding out of the throne of God and of the Lamb.
Word Study
Revelation — Salt Lake City: Deseret Book Co., 1998
Donaldson Commentary

These features are reminiscent of the Garden of Eden

proceeding out of the throne of God and of the Lamb. Ezekiel saw waters flowing from beneath the temple, or house of God. Here they flow from the throne, which might represent the holy of holies in the heavenly temple. The waters of life, then, or the waters of love, come forth from God's throne, from the heart of God's holy city. The symbolism suggests that these waters come directly from God himself. (Donald W. Parry and Jay A. Parry, Understanding the Book of Revelation [Salt Lake City: Deseret Book Co., 1998], 299)

2In the midst of the street of it, and on either side of the river, [was there] the tree of life, which bare twelve [manner of] fruits, [and] yielded her fruit every month: and the leaves of the tree [were] for the healing of the nations.
Word Study
Revelation — Salt Lake City: Deseret Book Co., 1998
Donaldson Commentary

"Then the angel showed me the river of the water of life, . . . flowing . . . down the middle of the great street of the city. On each side of the river stood the tree of life, bearing twelve crops of fruit, yielding its

fruit every month" (NIV).

This passage suggests that each tree bears a different fruit every month, for a total of twelve varieties each year. Further, unlike most fruit trees, which bear their fruit seasonally, the fruit of the tree of life is ever available. The tree of life is always living and growing, never subject to death, not even subject to the normal sequence of a winter's "death," a spring's sprouting, a summer's growth, an autumn's harvest, and another winter's death. (Donald W. Parry and Jay A. Parry, Understanding the Book of Revelation [Salt Lake City: Deseret Book Co., 1998], 300)

3And there shall be no more curse: but the throne of God and of the Lamb shall be in it; and his servants shall serve him:
Word Study
Revelation — Salt Lake City: Deseret Book Co., 1998
Donaldson Commentary

there shall be no more curse. This passage seems to refer to the curses that were pronounced on Adam and Eve at the time of the Fall-that they would "surely die" by partaking of the forbidden fruit, that the woman would bear children in sorrow, and that the man would eat his bread "in the sweat of thy face." The ground itself, in fact, was "cursed . . . for thy sake; in sorrow shalt thou eat of it all the days of thy life" (Gen. 2:17; 3:16, 17, 19). Those in the celestial world would be subject to these curses no more. It is interesting to note that the Bible begins with a record of humankind being cursed and ends with a vision of those curses being removed. (Donald W. Parry and Jay A. Parry, Understanding the Book of Revelation [Salt Lake City: Deseret Book Co., 1998], 301)

Donaldson Commentary

they shall see his face. One great purpose of the temple is to bring us into the presence of God. Much of the symbolism of the temple ordinances demonstrates how we can find our way back to him. 10And he saith unto me, Seal not the sayings of the prophecy of this book: for the time is at hand.
Word Study
Revelation — Salt Lake City: Deseret Book Co., 1998
Donaldson Commentary

Seal not the sayings of the prophecy of this book. Daniel was given a vision and told to seal it up "to the time of the end" (Dan. 8:26; 12:4, 9)-it was not the Lord's purpose for the world to know the things Daniel received. Earlier in John's vision, he is told to "seal up those things which the seven thunders uttered, and write them not" (10:4). Perhaps the time has not yet come for these specific things to be revealed. But the rest of the prophecy was intended for the world and the Saints, and John was commanded not to seal it up but to let it go forth for others to read and to seek to understand. (Donald W. Parry and Jay A. Parry, Understanding the Book of Revelation [Salt Lake City: Deseret Book Co., 1998], 306)

11He that is unjust, let him be unjust still: and he which is filthy, let him be filthy still: and he that is righteous, let him be righteous still: and he that is holy, let him be holy still.
Word Study
Revelation — Salt Lake City: Deseret Book Co., 1998
General Conference
; D&C 88:35). The same would be true of “selfish” or “disobedient” or any other personal attribute inconsistent with the requirements of God. Referring to the “state” of the wicked in the Final Judgment, Alma explains that if we are condemned by our words, our works, and our thoughts, “we shall not be found spotless; … and in this awful state we shall not dare to look up to our God” (Alma 12:14). — Dallin H. Oaks, in Conference Report, Oct. 2000, 41; or Ensign, Nov. 2000, 32
Donaldson Commentary

He that is unjust, let him be unjust still/he that is holy, let him be holy still. What is true of a person's spirit as it moves from this life to the next is also true of the person as he or she faces the time of the second coming of Christ. If we procrastinate the day of our repentance until the Lord's coming, we will continue in our filthiness after he arrives-and, likewise, the righteous and holy will continue in their holiness. Jacob taught the same principle, using some of the same words, in reference to our state at the final judgment: "They who are righteous shall be righteous still, and they who are filthy shall be filthy still" (2 Ne. 9:16; Alma 7:21; 41:10; D&C 88:35). (Donald W. Parry and Jay A. Parry, Understanding the Book of Revelation [Salt Lake City: Deseret Book Co., 1998], 306-307)

The prophet Nephi describes the Final Judgment in terms of what we have become: “And if their works have been filthiness they must needs be filthy; and if they be filthy it must needs be that they cannot dwell in the kingdom of God” (1 Nephi 15:33; italics added). Moroni declares, “He that is filthy shall be filthy still; and he that is righteous shall be righteous still” (Mormon 9:14; italics added; see also Revelation 22:11–12; 2 Nephi 9:16; D&C 88:35). The same would be true of “selfish” or “disobedient” or any other personal attribute inconsistent with the requirements of God. Referring to the “state” of the

wicked in the Final Judgment, Alma explains that if we are condemned by our words, our works, and our thoughts, “we shall not be found spotless; … and in this awful state we shall not dare to look up to our God” (Alma 12:14). (Dallin H. Oaks, in Conference Report, Oct. 2000, 41; or Ensign, Nov. 2000, 32)

Donaldson Commentary

“Blessed are they who do His Commandments.” Many years ago there was a story told in one of our early school books about some young people who were sailing down the river towards Niagara Falls. A man on the shore cried out to them: “Young men, Ahoy, the rapids are below you!” But they heeded not his warning call until they realized too late that they were in the midst of the rapids. With all the power at their command they failed to turn their boat upstream, “So,” said the man who tried to warn them, “shrieking and cursing, over they went!” The lesson left an indelible impression upon me; but today it seems incomplete. It is one thing to stand on the shore and cry, “Young men, Ahoy—there is danger ahead,” and it is another thing to row into the stream, and, if possible, get into the boat with the young men, and by companionship, by persuasion, by legitimate force, when necessary, turn the boat from the rapids. Too many of us are satisfied to stand on the shore and cry, “There is danger ahead.” (David O. Mc Kay, in Conference Report, Apr. 1964, 4; or Improvement Era, June 1964, 444)

John the Revelator, speaking of the tree of life in the New Jerusalem that is to come down from heaven, said: "Blessed are they that do his commandments, that they may have right to the tree of life, and may enter in through the gates into the city." (Revelation 22:14.) (Delbert L. Stapley, in Conference Report, Apr. 1966, 25; or Improvement Era, June 1966, 505)

15For without [are] dogs, and sorcerers, and whoremongers, and murderers, and idolaters, and whosoever loveth and maketh a lie.
General Conference
When the final judgment comes they are they who remain “filthy still.” They cannot enter the holy city, they are the “dogs, and sorcerers, and whoremongers, and murderers, and idolators, and whosoever loveth and maketh a lie,” who are cast out. (Rev. 22:15 [Revelation 22:15].) — Joseph Fielding Smith, in Conference Report, Oct. 1965, 28; or Improvement Era, Dec. 1965, 1107
Donaldson Commentary

For without are dogs. " `Dogs' is a traditional Jewish designation of heathen Gentiles." [Harrington, Revelation, 223.] The dogs, or the unbelievers, must stay outside ("without") the city—they are not blessed with celestial glory.

How wonderful is the peace and joy which fills the souls of the virtuous! How terrible are the torments of the unvirtuous! They shall have no place in the first resurrection. When the final judgment comes they are they who remain “filthy still.” They cannot enter the holy city, they are the “dogs, and sorcerers, and whoremongers, and murderers, and idolators, and whosoever loveth and maketh a lie,” who are cast out. (Rev. 22:15 [Revelation 22:15].) (Joseph Fielding Smith, in Conference Report, Oct. 1965, 28; or Improvement Era, Dec. 1965, 1107)

McConkie / Teachings of Joseph Smith
Murderers are forgiven eventually but only in the sense that all sins are forgiven except the sin against the Holy Ghost; they are not forgiven in the sense that celestial salvation is made available to them. (Matt. 12:31-32; Teachings, p. 356-357.) After they have paid the full penalty for their crime, they shall go on to atelestial inheritance. (Rev. 22:15.)McConkie, Mormon Doctrine
But at the Second Coming of the Lord sorcerers will be destroyed (Mai. 3:5; 3 Ne. 24:5); they shall be cast into that hell which is prepared for them (D. & C. 63:17; Rev. 21:8); and finally, having paid the utmost farthing for their crimes, they shall be debased with a telestial inheritance in eternity. (D. & C. 76:103; Rev. 22:15.)McConkie, Mormon Doctrine

Donaldson Commentary

My declaration is that this is precisely what the gospel of Jesus Christ offers us, especially in times of need. There is help. There is happiness. There really is light at the end of the tunnel. It is the Light of the World, the Bright and Morning Star, the “light that is endless, that can never be darkened.” 3 It is the very Son of God Himself. In loving praise far beyond Romeo’s reach, we say, “What light through yonder window breaks?” It is the return of hope, and Jesus is the Sun. 4 To any who may be struggling to see that light and find that hope, I say: Hold on. Keep trying. God loves you. Things will improve. Christ comes to you in His “more excellent ministry” with a future of “better promises.” He is your “high priest of good things to come.” (Jeffrey R. Holland, in Conference Report, Oct. 1999, 45; or Ensign, Nov. 1999, 36)

We could-and should-remember the wonderful things that have come to us in our lives and that "all things which are good cometh of Christ" (Moroni 7:24). Those of us who are so blessed could remember the courage of those around us who face more difficulty than we but who remain cheerful, who do the best they can, and trust that the Bright and Morning Star will rise again for them-as surely He will do (see Revelation 22:16). (Jeffrey R. Holland, in Conference Report, Oct. 1995, 90; or Ensign, Nov. 1995, 69)

Only Christ can be our ideal, our "bright and morning star" (Revelation 22:16). Only he can say without any reservation: "Follow me; learn of me; do the things you have seen me do. Drink of my water and eat of my bread. I am the way, the truth, and the life. I am the law and the light. Look unto me and ye shall live. Love one another as I have loved you" (see Matthew 11:29; 16:24; John 4:13-14; 6:35, 51; 7:37; 13:34; 14:6; 3 Nephi 15:9; 27:21). (Howard W. Hunter, in Conference Report, Apr. 1994, 83; or Ensign, May 1994, 64)

McConkie / Teachings of Joseph Smith
Christ is the Bright and Morning Star. (Rev 22:16.) In speaking of a man as a star the meaning is that he is a person of brilliant qualities, who stands out pre-eminently among his fellows. Thus to single out our Lord as the Bright and Morning Star, the last bright luminary of the night to give way before the rising sun, is to testify that he is pre-eminent over all his brethren, that he is the Son of God in whom all fulness and perfection dwell.McConkie, Mormon Doctrine
Thus "God is love" (1 John 4:8), and "God is light." (1 John 1:5.) Christ is "the way, the truth, and the life" (John 14:6), "the resurrection, and the life" (John 11:25), "the life and the light of the world" (D. & C. 11:28), "the Word" (John 1:1), and "the law." (3 Ne. 15:9.) Revelation Often Excluded A very rich irony is that this "claimed-canon-closer" Revelation passage was often itself considered not to be scripture! Revelation can quite confidently be called probably the most disputed book of the Bible. Ranging from expressing skepticism to calling Revelation a fraud, its early cynics included Origen, Eusebius — the father of church history

Donaldson Commentary

For I testify unto every man that heareth the words of the prophecy of this book. John here is not bearing testimony to the words of the book but is pronouncing a curse under the direction of the Spirit. The curse is that if any man adds to or takes away from what John has recorded, seeking to alter the scripture itself, that man will receive great punishment from God. (Donald W. Parry and Jay A. Parry, Understanding the Book of Revelation [Salt Lake City: Deseret Book Co., 1998], 309)

If any man shall add unto these things. "Of course a careful reading of this text shows very clearly that John the Revelator was speaking only of the book of Revelation and not of any collection of other sacred writings. Moses used a similar expression in speaking to ancient Israel when he said: `Ye shall not add unto the word which I command you, neither shall ye diminish ought from it, . . .' This is found in the fourth chapter of the book of Deuteronomy (verse 2). In the 12th chapter of the same book Moses said this: `What thing soever I command you, observe to do it: thou shalt not add thereto, nor diminish from it.' (Verse 32.) "Can anyone suppose that in these words Moses laid down a prohibition against all subsequent

revelations and against all books which might be called scripture in years to come? Did he have the power to silence all future prophets and forbid them to speak or write as God intended that they should? Of course not, or we would be without most of the Old Testament and would have none of the New Testament at all. "It was the same with John the Revelator. In warning against additions to the book of Revelation he spoke of that book only, insisting that no one attempt to change or corrupt what he had said. The Bible was not compiled when John wrote the book of Revelation, so he could not possibly have referred to it. Furthermore, scholars tell us that the Gospel of John was written after the book of Revelation, and if this be true it becomes another indication that John had no thought of precluding other writings but only of protecting this particular book of Revelation from change or corruption." (Mark E. Petersen, Conference Report, October 1964, 121.)

Question about Bible and Book of Mormon Recently a young friend in the mission field wrote a letter to me regarding a question that had been asked of him concerning the concluding verses of the Bible and how they apply to the Book of Mormon. We remember that at the end of the book of Revelation, the last book of the Bible, the author, John, issues a warning and a curse upon any man who adds to or takes away from the book. Specifically, these are the words he wrote: "For I testify unto every man that heareth the words of the prophecy of this book, If any man shall add unto these things, God shall add unto him the plagues that are written in this book: "And if any man shall take away from the words of the book of this prophecy, God shall take away his part out of the book of life, and out of the holy city, and from the things which are written in this book." (Revelation 22:18-19.) These verses of scripture have been cited repeatedly by those attempting to discredit the Book of Mormon, claiming that God's revelation to man is closed. Nothing more is to be added and nothing is to be taken away. They assert that the Book of Mormon is an attempt to add to the words of the Bible. These claims were made when the Book of Mormon was first published and have continued to be made, and are made today. Is there any validity to such assertions? Warning applies only to book of Revelation The answer to this query is really very simple. A careful reading of the words makes it clear that the warning against adding to or taking away does not refer to the whole Bible or even to the New Testament, but to use John's words, only to the words of "the book of this prophecy." That is, the prophecy contained in the book of Revelation. This is substantiated by the fact that some of the books of the New Testament had not yet been written when John wrote the book of Revelation, and even those that had been written and were in existence at that time had not yet been gathered into one compilation. The collection of writings consisting of the sixty-six books we know as the Bible were brought together and compiled into one volume long after John wrote the prophetic book that has been placed at the end of the collection. It is clear, therefore, that the terrible judgments pronounced upon those who add to the book could not possibly apply to the whole of the Bible or even to the New Testament, but only to the book of Revelation. John wrote other scriptures Secondly, the warning uses the words "the prophecy of this book" and also "the words of the book of this prophecy." The word book in both instances is singular and could only refer to the book of prophecy written by John which is titled, in the King James Version, "The Revelation of St. John the Divine" and is often referred to as the Apocalypse-a Greek word which means revealed. Of necessity the word book would have been in the singular because when written it was not associated with any other book or books, and it was after many years and many ecclesiastical debates that it was added to the collection that became known as the new canon of scripture or the New Testament. It is also interesting to note that John himself added to scripture after [CR, 87] writing the book of Revelation, which is generally conceded to have been written while he was on the Isle of Patmos. It was long after John left Patmos that he wrote his first epistle. This fact standing alone would be sufficient to

defeat the claim that revelation was closed and that man was enjoined from adding to scripture. This adds cumulative evidence that John had reference to the book of Revelation only. Admonition in Deuteronomy In the Old Testament also are found similar vigorous denunciations and commands that there shall not be taken away or added to the words that were written. The first is found in Deuteronomy, written at the time Moses was exhorting Israel to live the law of the Lord. The Torah was oral law and had not been reduced to writing prior to the time of the codification of the law in Deuteronomy. Now that it had been reduced to writing by Moses prior to his death and assumed to be complete, Moses wrote: "Ye shall not add unto the word which I command you, neither shall ye diminish ought from it, that ye may keep the commandments of the Lord your God which I command you." (Deuteronomy 4:2.) Later in this same book of the law, Moses repeated the admonition in similar words. He said, "What thing soever I command you, observe to do it: thou shalt not add thereto, nor diminish from it." (Deuteronomy 12:32.) In the minds of some, these admonitions in the Old Testament raise the same question as to the Book of Mormon being an attempted addition to scripture as does the injunction and warning at the end of the book of Revelation. In effect, these passages contain the same injunction as the one at the close of the Apocalypse; and if the same interpretation and argument was applied to them as is applied to the closing verses of the book of Revelation, there would be no scripture after the writings of Moses. Such an absurdity would result in discarding the greater part of the Old Testament and all of the books of the New Testament. Revelation guides prophets and Church A careful reading of each of these admonitions makes it clear that man is not to make changes in the revelations of the Lord: man is not to add to or take from the words of God. There is no indication or intimation that God could not, or would not, add to or take from; nor would any reasonable person with a belief in the divine powers of God consciously believe that God would be so restricted. Without question he would have the right and power to give additional revelation for the guidance of his children in any age and to add additional scripture. A study of the revelations of the Lord in holy writ confirms the fact that it is continuous revelation that guides prophets and the Church in any age. Were it not for continuous revelation, Noah would not have been prepared for the deluge that encompassed the earth. Abraham would not have been guided from Haran to Hebron, the Land of Promise. Continuous revelation led the children of Israel from bondage back to their promised land. Revelation through prophets guided missionary efforts, directed the rebuilding of Solomon's temple, and denounced the infiltration of pagan practices among the Israelites. Before the ascension of Christ, he promised the remaining eleven apostles, "lo, I am with you alway, Even unto the end of the world." (Matthew 28:20.) Following his ascension, he guided the Church by revelation until the death of the Apostles and subsequent apostasy of the Church of Jesus Christ. A distinctive sign of the last days that will precede the eventual second coming of the Lord was seen in vision by that same Apostle who recorded the book of Revelation. He said: "I saw another angel fly in the midst of heaven, having the everlasting gospel to preach unto them that dwell on the earth, and to every nation, and kindred, and tongue, and people." (Revelation 14:6.) The fact that John saw a messenger from God reveal anew a lost gospel negates the argument that further revelation could not be added to the Bible. (Howard W. Hunter, in Conference Report, Apr. 1981, 86-88; or Ensign, May 1981, 64-65)

How can one believe the Book of Mormon to be scripture when the Bible says we shouldn't add to the Bible (Revelation 22:18-19)? The phrases "the prophecy of this book," "the book of this prophecy," and "this book" refer only to the book of Revelation. At the time John recorded those words, the Bible did not exist; there were only separate scrolls for each book. The first bound Bibles do not appear until the fourth century A.D. Some of the earliest Bible manuscripts do not end with the book of Revelation and, in fact, some of them don't even include that book and omit other New Testament books as well.

Moreover, similar warnings are found in earlier Bible books. For example, in Deuteronomy 4:2, we read, "Ye shall not add unto the word which I command you, neither shall ye diminish ought from it, that ye may keep the commandments of the Lord your God which I command you" (see also Deuteronomy 12:12). While mortals are forbidden to add to the divine word, the Lord himself is not bound by such restrictions. God's word is revealed through prophets, and it is through prophets that he has always added to his own word. Thus, we read that, when the king had burned the words written by Jeremiah (Jeremiah 36:1-4, 23), the Lord commanded him to restore these words (Jeremiah 36:27-28). "Then took Jeremiah another roll, and gave it to Baruch the scribe, the son of Neriah; who wrote therein from the mouth of Jeremiah all the words of the book which Jehoiakim king of Judah had burned in the fire: and there were added besides unto them many like words" (Jeremiah 36:32). (FARMS answer of the Week)

REVELATION 22:18-19 – THE MORMON-THEOLOGY-KILLER PASSAGE

(If one is quickly persuaded or dissuaded by the premise about this passage, one may still take interest in the great material on the

plausibility of extra-Biblical revelation, or the simply superb material on Bible history. Send comments to sdrennan@juno.com)

INTRIGUINGLY INTERESTING INTRODUCTION

Arguably the most universal objection to The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints (LDS or Mormon Church) is the very widespread belief that there cannot be new scripture and prophetic revelation. And the scripture so quickly quoted to buttress this belief and to discredit and denounce the Book of Mormon is the extraordinarily famous end-of-the-Bible passage of Revelation 22:18-19:

18: "For I testify unto every man that heareth the words of the prophecy of this book, If any man shall ADD unto these things,

God shall ADD unto him the plagues that are written in this book:

19: And if any man shall TAKE AWAY from the words of the book of this prophecy, God shall TAKE AWAY his part out

of the book of life, and out of the holy city, and from the things which are written in this book."

No other scripture is so repeatedly used against the LDS church. Similarly, endless objections to LDS doctrine are based on an "it's-not-in-the-Bible" argument. And in vivid contrast to most Christian churches whose essence lies in professing to be the best at understanding the Bible, the LDS Church most earnestly professes to be THE church of Christ divinely restored and directed via revelation -- thus ongoing revelation and scripture are core to LDS theology -- if they are false, all LDS theology is false, and vice versa. And lastly, religious truth is the most important pursuit in the universe. So if the Revelation passage is the core scripture behind the core objection to the core of LDS beliefs on the core subject of the universe, it deserves an acutely hard-core review by any student of the issue, pro or con. (So this and otherwise being bored in a London hotel are my apology for the following loquacious

length.) This objection does have much more basis than just scriptural however. While churches are full of important truths and good Christians, people realize that there can be only one or none that is God's that He directly runs by revelation. Plus almost all churches make no claim to have prophets giving scripture and revelation. Hence, independent of any scripture, skepticism to modern prophets and new scripture is most understandable! Defining Scripture

Before reviewing the plausibility (or lack thereof) of additional scripture, "scripture" itself needs better definition. The term sometimes refers to what writings are accepted, recognized, or decreed as divine. However, the relevant usage here refers to what communication (not necessarily written) is actually divine and is of important general use (II Tim. 3:16). Divine means the words are either: 1.) in the first person from God; 2.) chosen by God though the wording may appear as if selected by the prophet; or 3.) selected by the prophet (though potentially with some inspiration on how to phrase or present). In this last category the message is still divine as the prophet is an authority on the issue due to having received revelation – so the doctrine is always right -- even if the wording, spelling, grammar, and possibly even irrelevant details might not be as desired by some people. The various writing styles of the different prophets might lead one to conclude that much scripture is of this third type. The other criterion is whether the revelation is of important general use. If someone received inspiration about where lost car keys were, this would not be of general usage. However, many individual stories and instructions are included because of their teaching benefit. Enough clarification -- the bottomline is that scripture means prophetic revelation, and that to deny additional scripture is to deny additional prophets and to deny additional prophetic revelation – this is an important point to keep in mind! Let the Great Friendly Debate Begin!

Enough introduction! People understand Revelation 22:18-19 to either mean that:

1.) Potential editors are warned about adding or taking away from the Book of Revelation

2.) Or as is much more commonly interpreted, that God will never again give more scripture other than the Bible

Thus with #2, any claim like the Book of Mormon must be the addition of man and therefore be false. It is my assertion that the evidence against this common interpretation is so immense that one cannot logically defend this view of this scripture, independent of one's religious beliefs and of one's beliefs about the plausibility of any particular claim to any post-Biblical revelation or scripture. In presenting this it is assumed the reader will interpret and enjoy its good-humored geniality, abundant alliterative artistry, recurring repetitive rhetoric, and healthy hint of humor all in the vivid vibrant vivaciousness intended -and not misinterpret a sincere serious studious spirited scholarly case as disrespect to anyone's beliefs. The following fifty points against this common interpretation fall into three broad categories:

A.) This common view is comprehensively and completely inconsistent with the Bible

B.) The Book of Revelation itself conclusively shows that John was speaking only about the Book of Revelation

C.) By reviewing Bible history, it becomes obvious the passage could not possibly have been referring specifically to the

Bible (even if it perhaps generically referred to all past/future scripture)

A. INTERPRETATION INCREDIBLY INCONSISTENT WITH THE BIBLE

A great variety of reasons show the common passage interpretation of no more scripture to be comprehensively and completely contradictory to the Bible. Problems with the passage itself will be reviewed first, inconsistencies with the rest of the Bible second.

1. Says What It Means, Means What It Says

Could the passage say what it means, and mean what it says? It warns against adding or taking away words. It simply never states the added common assumptions that God will never again speak, thus there can be no more scripture, and that therefore any scripture claim after Revelation is only man's false addition. In Exodus it also says to not bear false witness. By the same "logic" one could say that what this really means is that God will never again speak, thus there can be no more scripture, and that therefore any scripture claim after Exodus is only man's false witness! Instead of speculative conjecture, we should assume the passage says what it means, and means what it says!

2. Warn Editors, Not Readers: Bank Fraud

If the intent is to warn readers that no new scripture will ever come, why list penalties to editors? For example, jail sentences (plagues) warn criminals (editors) not to print counterfeit money (added text) or embezzle (take away), but are irrelevant to banks (readers) trying to ascertain which bills are counterfeit (added words) or which methods best recover money lost from fraud (text taken away).

3. Warn Editors, Not Readers: Sentence Analysis

By analyzing the sentence the focus on punishments becomes even clearer. A succinct summary is: "If you add, punishment; if you delete, punishment, punishment, punishment." The core clauses are about punishments. Most of the words are in the punitive phrases. A scare-them-straight speech is to warn potential perpetrators, not violated victims, to warn would-be writers, not the "scripture-swindled".

4. Warn Editors, Not Readers: "Oops! God Made a Mistake?"

In the common "logic", the take-away phrase is to warn readers words might be taken away. But readers would never learn of a text take-away, and if they did, they would always know the deletion to be due to man, not God! This take-away warning only makes sense if intended to warn an editor. Would one ever believe that God had made a mistake and now, oops, is taking away His own prior words?

5. Warn Editors, Not Readers: Triple Take-Away Focus

There are three warnings against taking away to only one against adding. If the prime objective was to let people know any claim of added scripture must be false, one might expect the larger focus to be on potential adds. However if the prime objective was to scare off would-be editors from deleting "objectionable" material they did not like, a triple-sized warning against taking away is understandable.

Modern Christianity offers parallels to ancient Christianity. Just as many modern Christian beliefs are not Biblical, so it was anciently. Just as deep Christian schisms exist today, so it was anciently. Thus the temptation to take away words that may lend credence to one's "theological enemies" may have been great for some. All of this leads to an understandably greater focus on warning against taking away.

6. Bible Adds Other Add Warnings – is our Take-Away to Take Away All Additions After the First Warning?

Deuteronomy 4:2 reads: "Ye shall not add unto the word which I command you, neither shall ye diminish ought from it." (Deut. 12:32 and Prov. 30:5-6 are also similar.) If we accept the "common logic", do we now take away all Bible books added after Deuteronomy?

7. Bible References Lost Verses of Scripture

James 4:5 reads: "Do ye think that the scripture saith in vain, The spirit that dwelleth in us lusteth to envy." John himself quoted Christ saying in John 7:38: "…as the scripture hath said…" and in John 17:12 "…that the scripture might be fulfilled." All these refer to verses of scripture not found in the Bible. Who is right – John, Christ, and the Bible, or those who say there can be no other verses of scripture?

8. Bible References Lost Books of Scripture

How can we say there are no other books of scripture when the Bible names them? Particularly when most of their authors are called prophets. (Seers are prophets (I Sam. 9:9)). An apologist may create an alternative explanation for a few of these, but not all let alone most:

1.) "The book of Nathan the prophet" (I Chr. 29:29, II Chr. 9:29)

2.) Referring to a fulfilled prophecy "is this not written in the book of Jasher?" (Josh. 10:13, II Sam. 1:18)

3.) Manasseh's dealings with God "are written among the sayings of the seers" (II Chr. 33:19)

4.) "Written in… the prophesy of Ahijah" (II Chr. 9:29, I Kings 11:29)

5.) An earlier epistle from Paul to Corinth (I Cor. 5:9)

6.) "The book of Gad the seer" (I Chr. 29:29)

7.) "The book of the wars of the Lord" (Num. 21:14)

8.) "Written in… the visions of Iddo the seer" (II Chr. 9:29, 13:22)

9.) "Read the epistle from Laodicea" (Epistle from Paul) (Col. 4:16)

10.) "The book of Samuel the seer" (I and II Samuel were not written by Samuel) (I Chr. 29:29)

11.) "The book of the acts of Solomon" (I Kings 11:41)

12.) "The book of Shemaiah the prophet" (II Chr. 12:15)

13.) A previous epistle from Jude (Jude 1:3)

14.) "The book of Jehu" (Called a prophet in I Kings 16:7) (II Chr. 20:34)

Who is right – the Bible or those who say there can be no other books of scripture?

9. Bible References Lost Prophets

The Bible lists other obscure prophets who left no known scripture -- Asaph, Eliezer, Hanani, Heman, Jahaziel, Jeduthun, Micaiah, Oded, and Zadok. Prophets reveal prophetic revelation. Who is right – the Bible or those who say the Bible recorded all revelation to all prophets? (II Sam. 15:27, I Kings 22:7-8, I Chr. 25:5, II Chr. 15:8, 16:7, 20:14-20,37, 29:30, 35:15.)

10. Bible References Lost Prophecies

How about prophecies quoted in the Bible but without any known source? For example Matthew 2:23: "That it might be fulfilled which was spoken by the prophetS, He shall be called a Nazarene." Jude 1:14 reads: "And Enoch… prophesied of these." John himself quoted lost prophecies (John 18:9, 18:32). Who is right – John and the Bible or those who say there are no prophecies outside the Bible?

11. Bible References Future Scripture

Ezekiel 37:15-20 prophesies about the stick of Judah – the Bible. (Jews wrote most of the Bible, Paul was called a Jew, and though Moses was a Levite the Jews kept his and all other books through the centuries.) Clearly the stick of Judah is the Bible. The passage then prophesies of the writing of the stick of Joseph and how that one day it would join the Bible. Isaiah 29 also tells of future scripture to be unsealed. (Both are fulfilled with the Book of Mormon.) Who is right – the Bible or those who say post-Biblical scripture cannot occur?

12. Bible References Future Revelation

Acts 3:20-21 says Christ will be in heaven "until the times of restitution of all things, which God hath spoken by the mouth of ALL his holy prophets since the world began" – the restitution of Christ's gospel and kingdom (Dan. 2:44) prior to His second coming. Obviously this prophecy list is way too long to review, but to sample Revelation itself -- "they shall prophesy" (Rev. 11:3), "two (future) prophets" (Rev. 11:10), and "another angel… having the everlasting gospel to preach" (Rev. 14:6). Since our debated passage is the New Testament closer, let us review the Old Testament closer: "I will send you Elijah the prophet before the great and dreadful day (second coming) of the Lord." So now Elijah cannot return as no more revelation is possible? Which "contradictory" testament-closer does one now choose?

13. Bible Implies Future Prophets

Three of the four Bible warnings about future false prophets are for the period prior to the Lord's second coming (Mark 13:22, Matt. 24:11,24). To discern between prophets, true or false, implies that some are true. If there were to be no more prophets, why not just say so?

14. Preparatory Pattern Broken?

The Messiah's initial coming had a preparatory prophet, John the Baptist, to prepare a people for the Savior. Similarly, the Messiah's second coming has had preparatory prophets (in the LDS Church). Does one now insist the Lord break His preparatory pattern?

15. Man's Pattern Broken?

The pattern of God's people is to drift from the truth. By Noah's time they were all wet. Israel's history is a recurring repetition of apostasy. Christians already adrift in the apostolic age are a big part of the epistles, even of Revelation 2 and 3. The Bible foretells Christian apostasy (II The. 2, I Cor.

11:18-19, Gal. 1:6-7, Mat 24:5,24, Acts 20:29-30, etc.). Embryonic Christianity is deep in discord. Political power helps create and sustain a "universal orthodoxy". This then splits East versus West in 1054. Eastern Orthodoxy then slowly divides, while in the West Protestants protest Catholic concepts. Protestants then protest Protestant principles, leading to hundreds of sects. However in many respects the most disconcerting deviations are within sects over time and within a membership at any point in time. Unfortunately for God's people there is a perpetual pattern of constant change – can one not see the tremendous need for revelation today?

16. God's Pattern Broken?

Throughout history when God has found a people that would work with him, it has been through revelation to his prophets. (Apostles are prophets who also serve as witnesses of Christ.) The record of this revelation is scripture. Amos 3:7 reads: "Surely the Lord God will do NOTHING except he revealeth his secret unto his servants the prophets." (Nothing means nothing – not a church, not a ministry, not a creed -- nothing means nothing!) So why would God change his pattern now? Indeed, how could God work with a people except by directing them by communicating with them, which is just another way to describe revelation!?!?!?

Some argue there was need for revelation after Christ but not after the apostles' demise. Were the apostles superior to Christ? Ephesians says the church is "built upon the foundation of apostles and prophets, Jesus Christ himself being the chief cornerstone" (Eph. 2:20, Eph. 3:5). Ask earthquake victims if foundations are no longer relevant to buildings. Also read II Cor. 12, especially verses 28-29.

Aside from revelation for salvation, what about for earthly needs? Would Peter have taken the gospel to the Gentiles on his own predisposition (Acts 10)? Would the Israelites have left Egypt by politely petitioning Pharaoh? Would Noah have built the ark on a lark?

17. Dramatic Departure Scripturally Supported?

If there were such a dramatic departure in God's pattern that he would never again give revelation, prophets, or scripture, why wouldn't God have said so? Such a radical new dogma based solely on a highly illogical reading of a single scripture – is this sound? Actually there are many scriptures referenced to also "support" this view, but they also rely on the same "logic" of baseless interpretations. However, these verses should be reviewed, as one of the strongest arguments for something is if the points used in opposition are weak. And if they are very weak and wrested (II Pet. 3:16), this greatly strengthens the main point! Some of the scriptures quoted for this view are Deut. 4:2, 8:3, 12:32, Prov. 30:5-6, Isa. 8:20, Mat. 4:4, 11:13, 17:5, John 15:15, 16:12-13, Acts 17:11, 20:20,27, I Cor. 13:8, II Cor. 11:4,13-15, Gal. 1:6-9, I The. 5:21, II Tim. 3:14-17, Heb. 1:1-2, II Pet. 1:3, 3:2,15-16, I John 4:1, and II John 9-11.

18. Doctrinal Diversity Sufficiently Specified?

The diversity of doctrines insufficiently specified in the Bible has caused a lack of unity within Christianity. If only a Bible is needed, then why were so many councils and conferences held to decide not only policy but also doctrine? Ephesians 4:11-14 states prophets and apostles are to assist in achieving unity and knowledge in a full and perfect manner, something Christianity with the Bible but without revelation has been unable to do. This is evident in Christianity's wide chasms on even the core principles of salvation.

For instance, how more basic an issue than the entrance to Christianity? The Bible repeatedly shows baptism to be a must (John 3:3-5, Mark 16:16, Luke 7:29-30, Matt. 28:19, Acts 2:37-47, Acts

10:46-48, Acts 22:16, Tit. 3:5, I Pet. 3:21). Is the Bible correct on this? Is immersion optional? Must it be by priesthood authority? What prayers are okay? If baptized in Christ's name, can any church do it, including the LDS Church? And if not, what criteria bar a church? What happens to good people who never heard of Christ and had no chance to be baptized? What sins annul salvation through baptism? Should infants be baptized? What happens to infants or the mentally handicapped who die unbaptized? Is rebaptism okay? And answer these fundamental salvation questions with only clear Bible verses!

The deep diversity of doctrine and dogma insufficiently and inconclusively specifically scripturally explained and expounded is marked and manifested by the vagueness and variety of theories and theologies among and amid the creeds and churches!!

Bible Consistency Summary

When one takes a scripture at face value, one should check their understanding by reviewing consistency with the rest of the Bible. The common interpretation of Revelation 22:18-19 fatally fails this test. First, and very fundamentally, because it is completely contrary to the face value meaning of the passage. Secondly, and very seriously, because it is incredibly inconsistent with the Bible!

B. RIGOROUS REVELATION READING REVEALS REFERENCE REFERS TO REVELATION ONLY

A careful analysis of this passage's wording and all of Revelation will clearly show the passage only refers to the Book of Revelation.

19. "THIS BOOK" Says What It Means, Means What It Says

A laborious look at all 262 modern KJV (King James Version) uses of the word "book/s" shows just a very few might be implied to mean all scripture, while the overwhelming majority clearly refer to a single book, and none imply a future Bible. (The closest might be Isaiah 29 as it prophesies future scripture, but clearly this could not have been the Bible, plus the Book of Mormon fulfilled these details.)

The phrase "this book" occurs 23 times – all of these have clear reference to something other than the Bible. For example, similar to our passage's "plagues that are written in this book", Deuteronomy 29 three times has the phrase "curses that are written in this book" – referring to curses listed in that same chapter. When John wrote: "And many other signs truly did Jesus in the presence of his disciples, which are not written in this book" (John 20:30) – John referred to his gospel, not some future Bible for which he is unaware of the content. Other uses of "this book" have action commands (to read, to place) – actions that could not have occurred if "this book" was some futuristic Bible. The New Testament has "this book" seven times, all written by John near the end of the individual book he was writing. Our passage under review says "this book" three times. There was no compiled Bible with a blank page at the end that John was writing on. During this era the scriptures were individual books. The word "Bible" comes from the Greek plural word "Biblia" which means "books". "This book" is singular – John was referring only to his Book of Revelation. "This book" says what it means, and means what it says!

20. "SEAL NOT" What Only You Have or a Nonexistent Compilation with Pieces Widely Copied & Dispersed?

Verse 10 of the final Revelation chapter reads: "And he saith unto me, Seal not the sayings of the prophecy of this book: (the exact same wording as in verse 18) for the time is at hand." There was no Bible at this time, plus John did not control the world's scriptures. It was impossible for John to seal or

release the Bible! Bill Gates can decide whether to release software he wrote, but he cannot decide whether to release all of the world's software that is already widely dispersed and that he did not write or even necessarily have a copy of!!! John would have failed at sealing the Bible just as Daniel would have had he tried to seal the Bible when the Lord told him to "seal the book" (Dan. 12:4). However John could have sealed Revelation just as he sealed another prophecy when Rev. 10:4 told him to: "Seal up those things which the seven thunders uttered, and write them not." These "seal" verses make it very clear our passage refers only to Revelation.

21. "The PROPHECY of This Book… The Book of This PROPHECY"

Within the New Testament only Revelation is primarily prophecy. Rev. 1:1-3 says this will be a book of prophecy. In Rev. 1:11 the Lord tells John "what thou seest, write in a book" and then 10 more times in Revelation John is told to write the prophecies, with once being told not to write one prophecy. The last chapter has a phrase: "The sayings of the prophecy of this book". Keep in mind "prophecy" and "revelation" are similar words. Similar to our passage's "book of this prophecy", Deuteronomy three times has "this book of the law" – Deuteronomy is a law book just as Revelation is a prophecy book. "The prophecy of this book" and "book of this prophecy" are not phrases that would be used for the Bible -- they clearly refer to Revelation.

22. "If Any Man Shall Add Unto THESE THINGS…"

John used the phrase "these things" 57 times in the Bible. From his gospel closer (John 21:24), John has 12 straight usages of "these things" associated with the words "write/wrote/written" -- all referring to John's specific writings. A tedious travail through all 57 of "these things" indicates the phrase always referred to items John was writing about – never the entire Bible.

23. "The PLAGUES That are Written in This Book"

Other than Apocalypse (other name for this book), "plague/s" is only mentioned four times in the New Testament, all in the context of an individual's disease needing to be healed. Yet in Revelation there are 12 mentions of prophesied widespread plagues, and countless verses describe the horrors of future gruesome plagues (Rev. 9, 16). "Plagues that are written in this book" clearly refers to the Book of Revelation.

24. "Take Away His Part Out of… the BOOK OF LIFE"

The phrase "book of life" is used eight times in the Bible, seven of which are in Revelation. We learn the book of life has the list of people that will go to heaven. The whole context of John's warnings is that of someone who has just read the Book of Revelation.

25. "Take Away His Part Out of… the HOLY CITY"

The Bible phrase "holy city" usually refers to Jerusalem, but this usage would not make sense here. Revelation 21 describes in splendorous detail a wonderful future holy city (heaven) for the righteous – this is the holy city referred to. John's context is all Revelation.

26. "Take Away His Part Out of… the THINGS Which are Written in This Book"

"The things which are written in this book" means just that – the phenomenal things promised in Revelation 21 and 22. If this phrase referred to "things" in the entire Bible, this would be vexatiously vague! Instead, it is referring just to the wonderful promises in Revelation.

27. "The Revelation…"

The only time the Bible capitalizes "revelation" is here in Rev. 1:1. It is capitalized as it is the name of the book. Thus it being described by "words of this prophecy", "things", and "things which are written herein" all have to refer to the Book of Revelation due to the capitalization. With these phrases being similar to phrases in our passage, this further reinforces that our passage refers to Revelation itself.

28. Starting and Stopping Statements

Expanding upon the previous point, when reading the opening and closing statements together (Rev. 1:1-3, 22:6-10, 18-20), the opening clearly refers to the Book of Revelation, and as the closing says similar things, this makes it even clearer our passage only refers to Revelation. Counting similar words/phrases, these verses have the following frequencies: 6 of prophecy; 10 of read/hear/see; 9 of things; 8 of this/these; 5 of "the time is soon"; 10 of sayings/words; and 7 of book. But for the full convincing impact, read the passages directly.

29. Colorful Contentious Controversy

Revelation's inspiration was probably the most disputed of any Bible book, but its meaning has been far more disputed. Symbolic enigmatic dramatic terrific-and-horrific apocalyptic prophecy naturally leads to prolific interpretive variety. Perhaps John's consciousness of its colorful content and capability to cause controversy was the reason he added the warning to this book but not his other scriptures.

Rigorous Revelation Review

A rigorous Revelation reading reveals this warning is about just Revelation. "This/these" five times of which three are "this book", four uses of uniquely Revelation topics, "prophecy label" twice, identical wording as with other phrases that refer to Revelation -- it is hard to imagine contriving a passage with more obvious reference to Revelation itself! And with verse 10 telling John to "seal not the sayings of the prophecy of this book", an impossibility to do with the Bible, what does become sealed is the doomed fate of this common interpretation.

C. HISTORY HELPS HIGHLIGHT HOW PASSAGE COULD NOT REFER TO THE BIBLE

Bible history clearly shows John could not have been referring to the Bible. Even if one thinks that perhaps John implied all scripture as opposed to just Revelation, a review of Bible history will show that John still could not have been referring specifically just to the Bible!

30. Canon Compilation Closer: Composition Chronology

People often assume the New Testament canon was compiled according to order composed, thereby indicating that the compilation-closer Revelation was written last, and that therefore John meant the entire Bible when he penned his passage. History shows otherwise.

Around 95 A.D. John wrote Revelation while on the Isle of Patmos (Greece, Rev. 1:9). John was banished to Patmos by Roman Emperor Domitian. After Domitian's murder in 96 A.D., John returns to Ephesus (Turkey), and writes his gospel. And John's three epistles are also believed to have been

written after he left Patmos. Some believe Jude was also after Revelation.

However caution is called for since uncertainty exists as to when almost all the New Testament books were written. While the above chronology of John's books is consistent with sources such as the Catholic Encyclopedia, many believe Revelation was the last book John wrote. On the other hand, some believe Revelation was written around 68 A.D., thus increasing the likelihood that Revelation was not the last book written. (It is believed the 27 books were written between 50 and 100 A.D.) Today's information age cannot sort out the historical order of writing -- it may have been even more difficult in the low-tech fourth century. While various chronological opinions exist, all bare no resemblance to compilation order. For example, one composition ordering, listed in compilation order, is 19(Matthew)/12/20/24/21/ 6/4/5/3/9/10/8/1/2/13/17/14/7/18/11/15/16/25/26/27/22/23(Revelation). Composition chronology theory now archaic and antiquated!!!

31. Canon Compilation Closer: Category Classification

The compilation may have been based on category classification. With the categories of historical (first, 5), epistles (second, 21), and prophecy (last, 1), last might be a natural choice for prophecy. (Within the epistles same-addressee groups are ordered first by author, then by general versus individual addressee, and then usually by descending length. The partial exception is Hebrews as Paul's authorship of it was disputed.) If prophecy was not chosen to be last, the next most logical place might be second. And indeed, the second most common location for Revelation was after the Gospels. (However, while the variety of book order in the various Bibles through the centuries has been very large, Revelation has usually been last.) The compelling category logic furthers decomposes the composition chronology theory!

32. Canon Compilation Closer: Confidence Crisis

Another factor on placement may have been lack of confidence in divine authenticity. Doubt can explain the placement of the last nine books. Seven because they were the most disputed books, the other two, I Peter and I John, because they were logically located with II Peter and II John. Of the challenged seven, Revelation was probably the single most disputed – thus potentially explaining its last place!

33. Canon Compilation Closer: Chronology of Consensus

Perhaps also key was the chronology of consensus acceptance. In broad terms, Christians accepted as scripture the Old Testament from the start, then the gospels, then Paul's epistles, then the other epistles, and finally Revelation. Often all New Testament books were accepted except Revelation – such as the Council of Laodicea decision (363) and the 85th Apostolic Canon (about 385). It would be natural to append to an accepted list a later further addition. Revelation being arguably the last book to be well accepted may have caused its last place!

34. Canon Compilation Closer: Creating a Chronicle

One possible element may have been an effort to create a chronicle of the events in order. This would explain the gospels first (Christ's life), Acts next (events shortly thereafter), then the epistles (instructional letters written decades after Christ), with finally Revelation last (future events). Compiling by event order instead of by writing order further diminishes the idea that Revelation was written last!

35. Canon Compilation Closer: Combination Criteria

Like the Old Testament, the New Testament order may be due to a combination of criteria. A separate category, most disputed, accepted last, event-chronology last – all of these combined may be why Revelation was usually placed last. Whatever the exact rationale was, clearly chronology of composition was irrelevant. Just as the previously quoted Deut. 4:2 is obscure, had Revelation been placed anywhere else, our prominent passage would never have become well known and this vexing verbosity would never have been written!

36. When Considered Scripture?

The New Testament books were not generally and uniformly thought of as scripture when John wrote Revelation! Aside from the big issues of which Christians were aware of and accepting of which books and when, a Christian would value an apostle epistle without considering it scripture! Decades after the apostles, as doctrinal disputes grew and copies proliferated, the books came to be more uniformly known of and called scripture. This pattern of gradually regarding a book as scripture also occurred for some of the Old Testament within Judaism. As reviewed before, in broad terms, Christians generally accepted the Old Testament from the start, then the Gospels, then Paul's epistles, then the other epistles, and finally Revelation. The first canon proposal we know of was about 140 A.D. (it rejected most of our current books), the next was about 180 A.D. – many scholars believe it was the last half of the second century when the New Testament books became generally considered scripture. Which books John was even aware of and which he thought Christians knew of and regarded as scripture is very unclear, but clearly it was not our present canon!

Similarly one could persuasively argue that John himself would not have thought II and III John (personal letters) would later come to be called scripture in a "Bible". For instance, III John is a short note to Gaius. Verses 1-8 are a personal complimentary and encouraging opening, 9-11 are negative remarks about a particular Christian leader, 12 is praise of one Christian, and 13-14 are a closing. Would John more likely badmouth a specific Christian leader in a book of scripture or in a personal letter? Unlike his gospel and Revelation, here the intent reads as a personal letter, not as scripture. This logic is one of the main reasons that II and III John were some of the books most anciently disputed. This dialogue does not challenge the inspiration behind the letters, but it does further challenge the idea that John's Revelation closer referred to a specific Bible where John was clearly aware of the canon that would go into it!

38. What Bible? From Penned Papyrus and Parchment to Printed Paper, From Several Scrolls to Countless Codices to a Bound Bible Book

The Bible is a relatively modern concept that clearly occurred after Revelation! The Old and New Testament writings within the Bible were written as individual scrolls. Early copies were also individual scrolls. (Interestingly, Revelation 5 has a sealed "book written within and on both sides" -- a rare "opisthograph", a scroll written on both sides.) The second longest of the 800+ Dead Sea Scrolls was Isaiah at 25 feet. Scrolls longer than this were rare as they would have been impractical. No scroll of the entire Bible has ever been found or heard of because it would have been 500 feet, and longer for Catholics! (Imagine being called upon in church to read a verse: "Yes pastor, but give me 10 minutes to scroll 400 feet to the passage...") John could not have had the notion of a Bible as a single book scroll!

In the second century some writings started appearing in a new form – the codex. The codex has pages written on both sides, usually bound by sewing. One source indicated that of non-Christian writings from Egypt, 2% were codices in the second century, 17% in the third, and 74% in the fourth. Christians were leaders in this new approach to books, and a far higher percentage of their books were in

codex form. Codices were more likely to be made of durable parchment/vellum (animal skin) than papyrus (an Egyptian reed). This material combined with being flat instead of an easily crushable scroll led to much more frequent survival of the codex. The codex, plus fourth century improvements in binding, facilitated the ability to append many writings together, thus giving us our oldest Bibles, two copies from the fourth century. But still almost all codices were individual books or small sets of books. One source counted 362 biblical manuscripts written before the tenth century, referring to only one as a complete New Testament and none as a complete Bible. (This defines "complete" by matching today's Bible, but not many come close by any definition.) This source also indicated that of the 5,366 known Greek biblical manuscripts, only 34 have the complete Bible, all of which were written after the year 1000. Wycliffe gave us the first English Bible – handwritten copies in the late 1300's. But printing and binding is what truly energized the idea of a Bible as opposed to just individual books! Gutenberg printed the first Bible in 1455 – 200 copies in Latin. Tyndale printed the first English New Testament in 1525. But not until the general era of the 1611 KJV did a Bible start to become much more common. John could only have been referring to the Book of Revelation -- the concept and particularly the commonness of a Bible as a single book occurred centuries after John!

39. Which Canon: Any Ancient Agreement?

Completely destructive to the idea that John referred to the Bible is that there never has been, including today, a single Bible canon. Which books belong in the canon has been debated for millennia. A review of some of the more famous ancient developments:

1.) Translated from Hebrew in the third century B.C. in Alexandria (Egypt), the Septuagint is the Greek Old Testament. The copies we have reflect a variety of wordings and canon. In general the canon is today's Catholic Old Testament.

2.) Reacting to Christianity, Jewish Rabbis endorsed a canon at a council at Jamnia (Israel) about 90 A.D. The main books that were questioned were Song of Solomon, Ecclesiastes, and Esther (the only book not in the Dead Sea Scrolls.) The canon was used by the Masoretic Text, the basis for the Protestant Old Testament. It did not include the Septuagint's Apocrypha.

3.) The oldest Old Testament documents are some of the Hebrew Dead Sea Scrolls that date to about two centuries before Christ. The oldest New Testament documents (no originals exist) date to about 125 A.D. – they contain portions of John in Greek.

4.) Marcion gave the first known Bible canon list about 140 A.D. – he rejected the Old Testament and much of the New Testament.

5.) The second and "more orthodox" list that we have is the Muratorian Canon, written about 180 A.D. It was the list of canon used at Rome – it excluded some of the modern KJV books, yet included others. Does one now consider as plague-worthy the forerunner of the Catholic Church? (Or from a Catholic point of view, the early Catholic Church?)

6.) In the early 4th Century famous "Father of Church History" Eusebius called some of today's New Testament books heresy. He may have been the first to compile a Christian canon into a single volume as around 330 A.D. the "colorful" and yet unbaptized Emperor Constantine requested Eusebius to produce 50 copies of the scriptures. However the canon selected is unknown.

7.) The oldest surviving Bibles are codices Vaticanus and Sinaiticus from the 4th Century, and Alexandrinus and Ephraemi from the 5th Century. These remarkable documents differed from each other in their canon, included some of the apocrypha in the New and Old Testaments, excluded some current

books and portions of books, and have been damaged causing lost pages.

8.) The first known tie to today's New Testament canon WAS NOT UNTIL 367 A.D.!!! It was part of a letter from Athanasius, bishop of Alexandria (Egypt). This list excluded books he had previously called inspired! His Old Testament list was close to today's KJV. This same New Testament list was also council approved at Hippo (Algeria) in 393 and Carthage (Tunisia) in 397 and 419, although these councils endorsed a different Old Testament -- the "Catholic" Old Testament. However, these were local synods in Africa, not general councils, just as Athanasius' letter was also only local!

9.) In 405 Jerome produced the Latin Vulgate, the level of effort he put into it varied widely by book. It was called Vulgate (from vulgar) as it put the Bible in more common Latin instead of a more old/formal wording. Ironically, Jerome did not want to include the extra "Catholic" Old Testament books but did so because he was requested to, thus in the Bible he gave them a disparaging preface. Plus Jerome at times favored other New Testament books that were excluded. Many centuries later his work became very popular. Today's Vulgate has as some of its primary sources Vulgate copies many centuries removed from Jerome. The Vulgate canon was finally officially accepted by the Catholic Church at the Council of Trent in 1546.

10.) The West eventually reached more similar canons, the influence of endless ancient canons impact Eastern churches even today.

11.) A Laodicean epistle, the Shepherd of Hermas, and Barnabas are among the books that were often added by many as scripture.

12.) Books excluded from the Bible are sometimes referred to as apocrypha (not capitalized), a further breakdown: Apocrypha (capitalized) if in the Catholic or Orthodox Bible but not today's Protestant Bible; New Testament apocrypha if of Christian origin; Pseudepigrapha if of Jewish origin; Dead Sea Scrolls; and Nag Hammadi Codices. (The latter two are recent finds.)

So which specific ancient canon was John supposedly referring to?

40. Which Canon: Catholic Canon Correct?

There were several early Christian variants, but eventually the Roman Empire's state religion of Catholicism became very dominant. Christianity owes Catholicism a tremendous debt for preserving the scriptures and for making Christianity the world religion! However the relevant question here is could Catholicism contain the correct, complete, and closed canon? From the Catholic Encyclopedia:

"The idea of a complete and clear-cut canon of the New Testament existing from the beginning, that is from Apostolic times, has no foundation in history. The Canon of the New Testament, like that of the Old, is the result of a development, of a process at once stimulated by disputes with doubters, both within and without the Church, and retarded by certain obscurities and natural hesitations, and which did not reach its final term until the dogmatic definition of the Tridentine Council."

What was this "dogmatic" council? Due to the Protestant "problem", the Pope convened the Council of Trent (Italy). On April 8, 1546 Catholicism for the first time officially adopted a certain canon and made it a matter of membership – the canon decree was voted in 24 to 15 with 16 abstentions. This vote may have had a very logical foundation, but does the following reflect an inspired canon decision:

1.) Canon being decided one and a half millennia later by a vote that could not get a majority (due to abstentions)

2.) What made this "stick" was the declaration that anyone not supporting it would be excommunicated and cursed

3.) The list excluded three books previously common, with many subsequent Catholic Bibles putting them in an appendix

4.) The Catholic Bible continued to undergo wording revisions, with some of the key changes in 1592 and the 20th Century

5.) The Bible decided upon descended significantly from Jerome's Vulgate, yet Jerome himself disagreed with the canon

6.) Pope Innocent (402-417) and Pope Gregory the Great (590-604) had different lists of scriptural books

Perhaps this may seem to be a serious scholarly selection, but should we deem it to be a divinely determined designation? 41. Which Canon: Perplexing Protestant Puzzlement?

Christianity owes a great debt to Protestantism for elevating the importance of the Bible and for seeking a more vibrant return to original Christianity. However the question here is whether the Protestant canon (also used by LDS) could somehow be the correct, complete, and closed canon. Canon closing is a particularly perplexing problem for Protestants. Protestantism's SOLE ancient source that ties precisely to its 66-book canon is very "mushy"! As Jerome disparaged the Apocrypha, by eliminating the Apocrypha from Jerome's Vulgate, this leaves the Protestant canon. Yet Jerome at times endorsed other books and vacillated on several existing New Testament books, plus he was a devout Catholic who almost became the pope!

The cynicism of Karlstadt, Luther, and Coverdale toward the "Catholic" canon of the Old Testament established Protestant ambivalence and ambiguity toward the Apocrypha. While most Protestant Bibles through the centuries have included the Apocrypha, they frequently gave them some sort of "second class" treatment. The "anti-Apocrypha" movement gained momentum in England in the mid 1600's. The KJV Bible retained the Apocrypha until 1796. But not until 1826 when The British and Foreign Bible Society recommended dropping the Apocrypha did most English Bibles include only "today's Protestant canon". And some still include the Apocrypha, such as the 1957 RSV.

This shorter Old Testament has its origins in the Council of Jamnia. Here Jewish scholars rejected the Apocrypha of the Septuagint in deciding a canon recommendation. A Protestant perplexity here is that this Jewish council was called as a negative reaction to Christianity.

Today some Protestants blithely repeat the Revelation passage completely unaware that their own canon: 1.) did not come about until 15 centuries after Christ; 2.) did not gain clear dominance within English Protestantism for three more centuries; 3.) is a minority canon (Catholics outnumber Protestants); and 4.) retained some books that the Protestant founding fathers were skeptical of. Yet this is the canon John supposedly referred to? And are Catholics and prior Protestants including their fearless founding fathers subject to plagues?

42. Which Canon: Obnoxious Obscenity?

Undermining the concept that any existing Bible canon is perfect is the Song of Solomon. The book is

0.000% doctrinal, historical, or religious. It is 100.000% love song, and then frequently obscene. While one could reference Jewish tradition for inclusion, a book too obscene for church cannot be taken as divine dogma for the religious instruction (it has not a verse, phrase, or word of religious content) of the church. Inclusion was debated anciently in Jewish circles, the wrong side won. Believe in scripture that is salacious but not religious?

With two probable exceptions, the New Testament books were all originally written in Greek. Thus Greek copies of New Testament verses should be quite accurate, since unlike translations, direct copies should be easy and consistent. Yet of the previously mentioned 5,366 Greek texts, no two of them have exactly the same wording except for a few of the very smallest fragments. Perhaps the first highly detailed review of variations, a study in 1707 of 100 different Greek documents found 30,000 variant readings in the New Testament. By another hundred years this number had grown fivefold. This is roughly 20 variant copy readings per New Testament verse found just by 1800! So which particular copy was John supposedly referring to?

Aside from copy variations, several books have great variety in text. (Not just individual words or phrases.) For instance Daniel, Esther, Acts, John, and Mark all have variations far beyond just "sloppy" handling. Text variations occur not only in ancient documents, but also in "mainstream" modern Bibles. For example, the last 12 verses of Mark are in the KJV and NIV, but not the oldest New Testaments or the 1957 RSV. Some of Acts was in most Bibles from the 6th Century through the KJV, but not in most of the oldest or modern versions. Ironically, even John and I John have variations – for instance John 7:53-8:11 is missing from many ancient and some modern Bibles.

At least today new Bibles follow various ancient texts or are otherwise ridiculed for not doing so. But in very initial Christianity the potential was much greater for a single change to cause most or all of the subsequent document problems to go undetected.

So which particular Bible text does one now say John was referring to?

45. Which Translation?

Translations into different languages cause significant problems. Counting similar editions, there are over 1,000 different English Bible translations -- literally almost every verse has variations! Similarly, the 8,000 plus Latin documents, though mostly copies of each other, are famous for their variety in wording. And the translations into the over 600 different languages and dialects frequently had less rigor than the English translations. Multiple factors affected the precise wording a translator settled upon:

1.) Which and how many different documents were reviewed, and a familiarity of the history behind these documents

2.) The level of understanding not only of various languages, but also of their idioms, literary styles, connotations, etc.

3.) Influence from sponsors, co-translators, other scholars, existing translations, one's own religious beliefs, and anticipated audience

4.) Style approach -- more literal or more believed tone/intent, more KJV reverential style or more

5.) The intelligence, judgment, attention to detail, frequency of errors, available time, and wording preferences of the translator

All of these factors impact the intellectually honest translator. So which of the endless Bible translations was John referring to?

Bible references to lost books were already reviewed. Additionally there is a plethora of apocryphal books, far more than most of us Christians are aware of. Some were considered anciently, others were found after the canon was largely settled. While most people believe most of this apocrypha is not divine, there is some material with good logic for being potentially inspired. We should realize the canon decision "process" wisely tried to generate consensus and avoid controversy. Books were more likely to succeed if there was a good degree of established acceptance as well as intellectual confidence. All of this means exclusion in error was more likely than inclusion in error.

47. Unknown Exclusion

Probably the larger exclusion is books that are simply unknown to us. For instance the gospels did not start to be written until three decades after Christ – scholars believe there were many prior writings of Christ's words as indicated in Luke 1:1-3. Also the Bible has no first-hand account today of anything until Moses (Moses wrote the first five books). Christianity owes a huge debt to the tribe of Judah for preserving the Old Testament for countless centuries and for preserving a people that followed many of God's truths. But do we Christians really think that the Pharisees, Sadducees, and scribes were divinely aware of all scripture in the history of mankind?

48. Unwritten Exclusion

Most inspired words of prophets were probably never recorded. But had they been, they would warrant inclusion in any complete list of scripture. Compared to today's technology, ancient writing was difficult, and copy dissemination much more difficult. Closing an epistle, John himself lamented: "I had many things to write, but I will not with ink and pen write unto thee: But I trust I shall shortly see thee, and we shall speak face to face" (III John 13-14). We have no scriptural record of these almost written "things". Closing his gospel, John speculated on other book possibilities: "And there are also many other things which Jesus did, the which, if they should be written every one, I suppose that even the world itself could not contain the books that should be written" (John 21:25). Also it is not the "Three Apostles" – certainly eight of the other original nine apostles must have said something during their decades of ministries!

49. Unimaginable Exclusion

The great and noble effort and endeavor of Protestantism over the last five centuries has been to follow the Bible as the absolute authority. This great focus on the Bible has greatly reenergized and strengthened Christianity. Without a church run by God, and with Christianity full of conflicting concepts, the Bible has logically been the sole source sought for authoritative answers. Thus a review of the preceding history can strike fear and panic in good Christian hearts – giving terror to a Christian that perhaps the Bible combined with his minister's understanding of it may not be sufficient to secure salvation. This horror of a thought simply closes many minds – surely God could not have left them without clear clarity for ensuring eternal exaltation. This potential exclusion is unimaginable! Yet this

"fearful reaction" is in clear contradiction with history. Countless billions have lived and died without hearing of Christ. Thus it must be possible that God would also allow people to die without understanding the complete gospel. (Though generic Christianity is strikingly deficient in explaining how a just and loving God could allow this to happen, fortunately modern revelation greatly expands a few Bible verses to provide a remarkable explanation. A partial answer is that people will have a chance to accept the full gospel after death but prior to the final judgment if they did not have a full chance in mortality – but the complete wonderful answer would require a tangential dissertation.) However common or understandable, close-mindedness due to fear is not a good method for ascertaining truth.

50. Unimaginable Endorsement

Similarly, churches are almost obligated to teach that no extra-Biblical revelatory wisdom is needed – for since they cannot offer it and do not claim to do so, it would be very difficult for them to recognize and admit the need for it. Does one expect an institution to teach against itself? Does Ford say GM cars are better? Do Republicans extol socialism as virtuous? Would President Clinton endorse Ken Starr? Could and would a creed or church teach and preach the need and necessity of a principle or practice it could not and cannot do or deliver? Opening Bible History: a Closing Summary

Should one desire a comprehensive canon review, an excellent choice would be Bruce Metzger's The Canon of the New Testament: Its Origin, Development, and Significance. For a good canon-complexity visual, a chart showing the books in each of over 100 different canon lists is at www.comdac.com/~trowbridge/canons.htm. I apologize for no references – this started from unexpected spare time in a hotel -- had I known this effort would have developed so much I would have kept them. Suffice it to say I meticulously weeded out dubious items from various sources, and even sometimes verified items by going to a copy of the Latin document. The bible history is the common summary that can be found in any thorough review, the Revelation details required more research. A very dominant amount of the information is from the works of Metzger, a preeminent Bible historian. The sources used were not LDS.

Had Revelation's order been anywhere else, this vexing verbosity would never have been written. When we realize that a Bible would not exist for centuries and would not become common for 1500 years, we realize John was not referring to a Bible. And with so many different canons, copies, texts, and translations, how could one ever interpret that John was referring to the specific Bible the reader was using?
